  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_bdc36fc5-cbb5-413d-92ac-a67d3c4f4a40'

  <span id="freeText12300752464564252775" style="display:none"><b>A timeless tale by the incomparable Kate DiCamillo, complete with stunning full-color plates by Bagram Ibatoulline, honors the enduring power of love.</b><br /><br />"Someone will come for you, but first you must open your heart. . . ."<br /><br />Once, in a house on Egypt Street, there lived a china rabbit named Edward Tulane. The rabbit was very pleased with himself, and for good reason: he was owned by a girl named Abilene, who treated him with the utmost care and adored him completely. <br /><br />And then, one day, he was lost. <br /><br />Kate DiCamillo takes us on an extraordinary journey, from the depths of the ocean to the net of a fisherman, from the top of a garbage heap to the fireside of a hoboes' camp, from the bedside of an ailing child to the bustling streets of Memphis. And along the way, we are shown a true miracle — that even a heart of the most breakable kind can learn to love, to lose, and to love again.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12300752464564252775" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      $('edition_2642151').observe('prototip:shown', function() {
        if (this.up('#box')) {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: $('box').getStyle('z-index')})});
        } else {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: 6000})});
        }
      });

      newTip['wrapper'].addClassName('prototipAllowOverflow');

        $('edition_2642151').observe('prototip:shown', function () {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function (e) {
            if ($('edition_2642151').hasClassName('ignored')) {
              e.setStyle({'display': 'none'});
              return;
            }
            e.setStyle({'overflow': 'visible'});
          });
        });
      $('edition_2642151').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();

        });

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">The china rabbit and china dolls in this story are unable to affect humans in any way other than to act as objects of comfort, so I suspect it would b<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">The china rabbit and china dolls in this story are unable to affect humans in any way other than to act as objects of comfort, so I suspect it would be a good story for you. It might even reassure you that china dolls do not pose any threat to you.<br /><br />On the other hand, if your fear is based on a suspicion that the china dolls are secretly able to see and hear you, you might not like it.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>

          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: 6000})});
        }
      });
      $('span_class_gr-hyperlink_sort_order_span_tip_724').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

//]]>
</script>    </div>

<div id="review_2548213390" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2548213390" />
    <a title="Mischenko" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1194517-mischenko"><img alt="Mischenko" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1587209348p2/1194517.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2548213390?book_show_action=true">Oct 04, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mischenko" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mischenko" href="/user/show/1194517-mischenko">Mischenko</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1194517-mischenko?shelf=middle-grade-children-s">middle-grade-children-s</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2548213390" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10503934418399818101">Oct. 14th 2020 - Another reread with my 7-year-old. I fell in love with this story all over again!<br/><br/>I picked up <i>The Miraculous Journey of Edward Tulane</i> after a high recommendation from a friend on Goodreads. We’d just finished reading and reviewing <i>Because of Winn-Dixie</i> which we loved. I took a chance on it and purchased it from Barnes and Noble to read with my kids.<br/><br/>The story begins with Edward Tulane, a China rabbit who belongs to a girl named Abilene. The family is wealthy and not only does Edwa</span>
  <span id="freeText10503934418399818101" style="display:none">Oct. 14th 2020 - Another reread with my 7-year-old. I fell in love with this story all over again!<br /><br />I picked up <i>The Miraculous Journey of Edward Tulane</i> after a high recommendation from a friend on Goodreads. We’d just finished reading and reviewing <i>Because of Winn-Dixie</i> which we loved. I took a chance on it and purchased it from Barnes and Noble to read with my kids.<br /><br />The story begins with Edward Tulane, a China rabbit who belongs to a girl named Abilene. The family is wealthy and not only does Edward have fancy clothes to wear, but he has the love of Abilene as well. She absolutely adores him and spends all of her time caring for him.<br /><br /><i>
  <b>In all, Edward Tulane felt himself to be an exceptional specimen. Only his whiskers gave him pause. They were long and elegant (as they should be), but they were of uncertain origin. Edward felt quite strongly that they were not the whiskers of a rabbit. Whom the whiskers had belonged to initially–what unsavory animal–was a question Edward cold not bear to consider for too long. </b>
</i><br /><br />Edward is really quite vain and wishes that the adults in the household would treat him differently, but at the same time he simply doesn’t realize what he has. He can’t talk or move, but he is very aware of the world around him. He listens to the humans as they talk, but he rarely wants to. Eventually Abilene’s grandmother shares a story with Abilene and Edward about a princess who refused to love anyone and was turned into a warthog by a witch. Edward isn’t sure why, but the story seems to be directed toward him. The grandmother then stares into his eyes and simply tells him, “You disappoint me.”<br /><br />It isn’t long after that Abilene’s father shares the announcement of a family trip to London. Abilene decides to take Edward of course, but after an incident with some boys on the ship, Edward is stripped of his fancy clothing, thrown overboard, and sinks to the bottom of the dark ocean. Naked and alone, he becomes scared. Feeling a true emotion for the first time, he then begins to question his future. What will happen to poor Edward now?<br /><br />From the very first pages I was pulled into the story. As an adult, I felt many emotions and even had to walk away at one point because I became over emotional in tears. We even laughed at times too. It’s one of those books that you simply can’t put down and my kids kept telling me, “We have to keep going!” It’s written well and the characters are truly unforgettable. Every character has their own story, but often the reader is left to draw their own conclusion which opens up plenty of discussion. When we finally got to the end, we didn’t want it to be over. With that said, I felt weird about the last few chapters at first, but as we finished it all came together. This is a book I could read over and over again–it’s one to keep!<br /><br />What an amazing author to write a book like this. Kate DiCamillo literally captured my heart again and I can’t wait to read all of her other books. She’s become a favorite author and we want to read everything she writes. I honestly question how this book didn’t win awards and wish they’d do a film adaptation.<br /><br />The book is obviously great for any age, but I personally felt like some of the vocabulary seemed a bit advanced for 1st and 2nd graders as the recommended age listed for this book on Amazon is 6-9. There were a few vocabulary words my 4th graders didn’t know. It might be best to brush over the more difficult words before reading. There is a free teaching guide on the author’s website.<br /><br />I’m choosing this book for my October read for the <b>Ultimate Reading Challenge</b>. One of my favorite tropes is when a character learns about the power of love and I feel this book is a great example.<br /><br />I’d recommend this book to anyone. Over the top 5-stars!<br /><br />See this review on blog @ <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://readrantrockandroll.com/2018/10/04/book-review-the-miraculous-journey-of-edward-tulane-by-kate-dicamillo-katedicamillo-ultimatereadingchallenge/">https://readrantrockandroll.com/2018/...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="10503934418399818101" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2616265119" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13860247990232624465"><blockquote>
  <i>"It is a horrible, terrible thing, the worst thing, to watch somebody you love die right in front of you and not be able to do nothing about it."</i>
</blockquote>Once upon a time, there was a poignant and philosophical tale about a solipsistic, egocentric china rabbit named Edward Tulane. The rabbit was loved by a little girl named Abilene. Albeit loved by his owner, he was incapable of loving others. One day, he was magically cursed by Pellegrina, the girl's grandmother who gifted her the narcissistic bunny</span>
  <span id="freeText13860247990232624465" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>"It is a horrible, terrible thing, the worst thing, to watch somebody you love die right in front of you and not be able to do nothing about it."</i>
</blockquote>Once upon a time, there was a poignant and philosophical tale about a solipsistic, egocentric china rabbit named Edward Tulane. The rabbit was loved by a little girl named Abilene. Albeit loved by his owner, he was incapable of loving others. One day, he was magically cursed by Pellegrina, the girl's grandmother who gifted her the narcissistic bunny. And so begins a miraculous journey full of deep dejections, pervasive melancholy, irrepressible yearning, and finally, finding true love once again.<blockquote>
  <i>"Open your heart. Someone will come. Someone will come for you. But first you must open your heart."</i>
</blockquote>Once, there was a rabbit who swore to himself that he would never love again. And yet, something happened in that expedition of his that changed his life...<blockquote>
  <i>"How many times, Edward wondered, would he have to leave without getting the chance to say goodbye?"<br /><br />"Edward knew what it was like to say over and over again the names of those you had left behind. He knew what it was like to miss someone."</i>
</blockquote>Once, there was a story — an extraordinary tour de force that broke and mended one's heart with the transformative power of unconditional and boundless love filled with sanguine hope. An author, by the name of Kate DiCamillo, penned an unforgettable narrative laden with fundamental themes, lyrical language, and masterful storytelling and thoroughly supplemented with splendid artwork painted by a wondrous illustrator named Bagram Ibatoulline — together, they created a breathtaking yet simple, imaginative story that warmed the hearts of adults and children alike. <br /> <br />Once, oh marvellous once, there was a reviewer whose heart has been touched by a china rabbit, he clutched this book against his broken heart and this simple tale alone filled his eyes with helpless tears. He swore to himself that no matter what happens, his heart must go on. And so, he found his way home, once again...<blockquote>
  <i>"The heart breaks and breaks and lives by breaking. It is necessary to go through dark and deeper dark and not to turn."</i>
</blockquote></span>

<div id="review_2008489639" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2008489639" />
    <a title="Cristina Monica" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14596170-cristina-monica"><img alt="Cristina Monica" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1589617514p2/14596170.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2008489639?book_show_action=true">May 23, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cristina Monica" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cristina Monica" href="/user/show/14596170-cristina-monica">Cristina Monica</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14596170-cristina-monica?shelf=children-s">children-s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14596170-cristina-monica?shelf=love">love</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2008489639" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3381963483506220721">I haven’t read a novel I absolutely loved from the very first sentence in a long time. I always say I have, probably because I want to believe that (and anyway, we all do it), but not every book is great and not every great book is exceptional. I want exceptional. Give me exceptional. <br/><br/>Kate DiCamillo’s <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/37186.The_Miraculous_Journey_of_Edward_Tulane' title='The Miraculous Journey of Edward Tulane by Kate DiCamillo' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Miraculous Journey of Edward Tulane</a> is just that. It tells the story of little china bunny Edward Tulane who knows not what love means. He is loved by a little girl, and yet, he doesn’t care. H</span>
  <span id="freeText3381963483506220721" style="display:none">I haven’t read a novel I absolutely loved from the very first sentence in a long time. I always say I have, probably because I want to believe that (and anyway, we all do it), but not every book is great and not every great book is exceptional. I want exceptional. Give me exceptional. <br /><br />Kate DiCamillo’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/37186.The_Miraculous_Journey_of_Edward_Tulane" title="The Miraculous Journey of Edward Tulane by Kate DiCamillo" rel="nofollow noopener">The Miraculous Journey of Edward Tulane</a> is just that. It tells the story of little china bunny Edward Tulane who knows not what love means. He is loved by a little girl, and yet, he doesn’t care. He’s self-centered and vain. But little Edward Tulane will discover what love means the way we all do: through loss… through life. <br /><br />The writing is simply delightfully lyrical. Is lyrical writing always delightful? Not quite, sometimes it doesn’t work. One must master the technique fairly well to pull it off. I used to think children’s books were so, so easy to write (‘‘anyone could write them!’’) but now that I’ve read a hundred of them and more, I realize that is not the case. Otherwise they would all be bestsellers. <br /><br />This is, without a single, lonely doubt, my most favourite Kate DiCamillo book. It’s also the one with the highest ratings on Goodreads, so I’m happy to see other people were positively, beautifully affected by this novel like I was. I think Kate DiCamillo can do no wrong, but she really surpassed herself with this one. <br /><br /><a href="https://hitormissbooks.wordpress.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">Blog</a> | <a href="https://www.youtube.com/c/LolaReviewer" rel="nofollow noopener">Youtube</a> | <a>Twitter</a> | <a>Instagram</a> | <a href="https://plus.google.com/u/0/105021852309973899120/posts" rel="nofollow noopener">Google+</a> | <a href="https://www.bloglovin.com/blogs/hit-miss-books-12564715" rel="nofollow noopener">Bloglovin’</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="3381963483506220721" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2414034" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2414034" />
    <a title="Lizzie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/150843-lizzie"><img alt="Lizzie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1182805593p2/150843.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2414034?book_show_action=true">Jun 26, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lizzie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lizzie" href="/user/show/150843-lizzie">Lizzie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">children, people who have/work with children, people looking for ways to explain the selfless love</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/150843-lizzie?shelf=childrensrereads">childrensrereads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2414034" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10264137052158941517">I looked forward to reading this book because, as the review on goodreads of <u>Edward Tulane</u> says, Kate DiCamillo is an incomparable children's author, and I have loved and cried over <u>The Tale of Despereaux</u> and <u>The Tiger Rising</u> in the middle of the Borders Cafe before. Edward's journey is miraculous in that the little china rabbit from which the book gets its name learns to love out of his many losses, which starts with losing his straw hat and ends with losing his hardened heart. Take the velvete</span>
  <span id="freeText10264137052158941517" style="display:none">I looked forward to reading this book because, as the review on goodreads of <u>Edward Tulane</u> says, Kate DiCamillo is an incomparable children's author, and I have loved and cried over <u>The Tale of Despereaux</u> and <u>The Tiger Rising</u> in the middle of the Borders Cafe before. Edward's journey is miraculous in that the little china rabbit from which the book gets its name learns to love out of his many losses, which starts with losing his straw hat and ends with losing his hardened heart. Take the velveteen rabbit, make him more fragile by making him the China rabbit, put in Kate DiCamillo's voice behind every word, and you have a new classic for children.<br /><br /> "I'm done with being loved," Edward told her. "I'm done with loving. It's too painful." . . . <br /><br /> "Open your heart," she said gently. "Someone will come. Someone will come for you. But first you must open your heart."<br /> The door closed. The sunlight disappeared.<br /> <u>Someone will come.</u><br /> Edward's heart stirred. He thought, for the first time in a long time, of the house on Egypt Street and of Abilene winding his watch and then bending toward him and placing it on his left leg, saying, "I will come home to you."<br /> No, no, he told himself. Don't believe it. Don't let yourself believe it.<br /> But it was too late.<br /> <u>Someone will come for you.</u><br /> The China rabbit's heart had begun, again, to open.<br /><br />That's not the end of the story, but it is such an important passage for so many people to read. I needed to read it the other day when I sat there in Borders once again, tearing up. I'll need to read it again someday, maybe soon. And I think it's worth it for most anyone to take 30 minutes at the most, sit down, and read about how a China rabbit learns to love. And then learns to love again.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10264137052158941517" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3303500203" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3303500203?book_show_action=true">Apr 28, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="HBalikov" class="user" itemprop="url" name="HBalikov" href="/user/show/929238-hbalikov">HBalikov</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3303500203" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12756459502047023135">I don’t question much of what Kate DiCamillo chooses to write about. Going with the flow has its rewards. I had my doubts whether our “five going on six” year old would have the same attitude, but she did. <br/><br/>She stuck with the story, day after day, as this haughty and aloof china and cloth rabbit went through his reversals of fortune. Edward begins by caring for no one...not even the little girl who loves and cares for him.<br/><br/>In DiCamillo’s world, dolls can think and feel and talk. We humans just can</span>
  <span id="freeText12756459502047023135" style="display:none">I don’t question much of what Kate DiCamillo chooses to write about. Going with the flow has its rewards. I had my doubts whether our “five going on six” year old would have the same attitude, but she did. <br /><br />She stuck with the story, day after day, as this haughty and aloof china and cloth rabbit went through his reversals of fortune. Edward begins by caring for no one...not even the little girl who loves and cares for him.<br /><br />In DiCamillo’s world, dolls can think and feel and talk. We humans just can’t hear them.<br /><br />Edward is very self-involved. He likes the fancy clothes in which the little girl dresses him. He likes the attention that he is paid by the family. Only the little girl’s grandmother seems to notice how little he gives back for what he is given.<br /><br />The story is about Edward’s journey of misfortune through many different “owners” and his experiences with how others survive or don’t in his world.<br /><br />DiCamillo’s magical realism worked for both of us as we waited each day for what would happen next. I hope you find it as engaging as we did. <br /><br />I should add that the illustrations are a fine match for the prose.<br />4.5*</span>
  <a data-text-id="12756459502047023135" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/41310561?book_show_action=true">Dec 30, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Erin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Erin" href="/user/show/857670-erin">Erin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/857670-erin?shelf=childrens">childrens</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer41310561" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12487150885032153518">Well-written, beautiful, and somber. This is the story of a china rabbit who learns the true meaning of love. I read this book out loud to my class and truly enjoyed the masterful way that Kate DiCamillo crafts a story. Her word choice and sentence fluency found its way into my writing lesson plans to illustrate some of the possibilities waiting to be found in words. <br/><br/>Would I read it aloud again? Perhaps with older students. My class LOVED this story but it was sad, extremely sad in places. I fou</span>
  <span id="freeText12487150885032153518" style="display:none">Well-written, beautiful, and somber. This is the story of a china rabbit who learns the true meaning of love. I read this book out loud to my class and truly enjoyed the masterful way that Kate DiCamillo crafts a story. Her word choice and sentence fluency found its way into my writing lesson plans to illustrate some of the possibilities waiting to be found in words. <br /><br />Would I read it aloud again? Perhaps with older students. My class LOVED this story but it was sad, extremely sad in places. I fought back tears while reading and had to let it sit for awhile before reviewing it because it dealt with some tough material. (Losing people you love.) Initially, I thought I would not recommend it nor read it again because it was just too strong. However, the more I considered this, the more I realized that to not recommend this book would be to make the same mistake that Edward made, and that is to refuse to experience something wonderful just because it can also be hard. So I do recommend it - I do! The lessons learned by Edward apply to us all. Just consider keeping a box of tissues close by. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12487150885032153518" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16731747-calista?shelf=want-to-own">want-to-own</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1373145878" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10136567040136423086">Typically, I adore everything Kate does. I thought this was a good story, but not one of her great stories. I didn't feel for Edward the way I normally do for her characters. <br/><br/>Edward is a porcelain toy rabbit. He doesn't appreciate being so well loved by his little girl until one day he's thrown overboard on a sea voyage and his miraculous journey begins. It's the type of book that he does get back to where he started after all hope seems lost. <br/><br/>The tone of the book felt melancholy to me. I never </span>
  <span id="freeText10136567040136423086" style="display:none">Typically, I adore everything Kate does. I thought this was a good story, but not one of her great stories. I didn't feel for Edward the way I normally do for her characters. <br /><br />Edward is a porcelain toy rabbit. He doesn't appreciate being so well loved by his little girl until one day he's thrown overboard on a sea voyage and his miraculous journey begins. It's the type of book that he does get back to where he started after all hope seems lost. <br /><br />The tone of the book felt melancholy to me. I never really cared about Edward for some reason. I guess I'm getting jaded in my older age. I knew when starting that he would make it back to his owner somehow and there were no stakes for me. I'm usually willing to go on the journey. I still thought it was a cute story, well done. <br /><br />This is a great story for children and I still recommend it. I guess, I simply expected too much from it. </span>

          </span>

    <a title="Sylvia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2486347-sylvia"><img alt="Sylvia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1246678716p2/2486347.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/184837625?book_show_action=true">Jul 13, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sylvia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sylvia" href="/user/show/2486347-sylvia">Sylvia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2486347-sylvia?shelf=reading-with-2boys">reading-with-2boys</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer184837625" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9817322425867022658">When a friend told me she was reading this book with her 3 and 5 year old kids, I immediately questioned whether they found it too sad. I purchased this book soon after its initial publication and had read it myself at that time. My recollection was that it brought me to tears, and as I have two quite sensitive little boys, I was hesitant to introduce this one to them. Inspired by my friends successful reading of it with her two (slightly) younger children, I bravely undertook this venture - for</span>
  <span id="freeText9817322425867022658" style="display:none">When a friend told me she was reading this book with her 3 and 5 year old kids, I immediately questioned whether they found it too sad. I purchased this book soon after its initial publication and had read it myself at that time. My recollection was that it brought me to tears, and as I have two quite sensitive little boys, I was hesitant to introduce this one to them. Inspired by my friends successful reading of it with her two (slightly) younger children, I bravely undertook this venture - for it was really me I was worried about being able to "handle" it. I was not able to hold back the tears as the story drew to a close, but my two beautiful boys wiped them away and assured me that there was nothing to be sad about, and I got the opportunity (one that seems to present itself quite often lately) to explain how tears can come from happiness as much as from sadness. A miraculous journey, indeed.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9817322425867022658" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

A little less magical, perhaps, but I appreciated the story of a rabbit who, instead of starting out full of love, learns to love and be loved until at the end of the story, he comes full circle. Easy reading, and could be a good one for reading out loud.</span>

<div id="review_2550708475" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2550708475" />
    <a title="Dannii Elle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44487975-dannii-elle"><img alt="Dannii Elle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508499419p2/44487975.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2550708475?book_show_action=true">Nov 05, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dannii Elle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dannii Elle" href="/user/show/44487975-dannii-elle">Dannii Elle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780763643676" href="/book/show/6552772-the-miraculous-journey-of-edward-tulane">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44487975-dannii-elle?shelf=magical-realism-mastery">magical-realism-mastery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2550708475" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2076442147842149104">This is one of those eternally relevant children's stories, that can be read by all ages throughout all of time. In fact, it <i>should</i> be read by all. I'm only now discovering this tale at the age of 28 and just know that if my younger self had managed to get her hands on it, it would have become a firm, forever favourite.<br/><br/>The reader travels with a china rabbit named Edward Tulane through, as the synopsis states, "the depths of the ocean to the net of a fisherman, from the bedside of an ailing child</span>
  <span id="freeText2076442147842149104" style="display:none">This is one of those eternally relevant children's stories, that can be read by all ages throughout all of time. In fact, it <i>should</i> be read by all. I'm only now discovering this tale at the age of 28 and just know that if my younger self had managed to get her hands on it, it would have become a firm, forever favourite.<br /><br />The reader travels with a china rabbit named Edward Tulane through, as the synopsis states, "the depths of the ocean to the net of a fisherman, from the bedside of an ailing child to the bustling streets of Memphis". Edward, over the course of his adventures, learns what love is about, and as he fell in love with all his many owners, I fell in love with him and his story.<br /><br />The ending is unbearably poignant and I was openly weeping with a mixture of many emotions, when I finished this. Don't let the age range for this wonderful story put you off reading it, it has as much to give any reader as any other children's classic.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2076442147842149104" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42508-greg?shelf=books-for-kids">books-for-kids</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42508-greg?shelf=girls-girls-girls">girls-girls-girls</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer62409376" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8467113249857899923">While sitting in Washington Square Park reading my <i>Moomins</i> novel while on a mini-lunch break I wondered about the recurring use of sadness and melancholy in the book. Do American children books usually refer to the the joys of a sweet melancholy feeling at the lose of something good that will never return? How do children relate to depressed characters? Do they even notice it? Why aren't more children books filled with melancholy, and not in a humorous way? While the sun beat down on my bench, a</span>
  <span id="freeText8467113249857899923" style="display:none">While sitting in Washington Square Park reading my <i>Moomins</i> novel while on a mini-lunch break I wondered about the recurring use of sadness and melancholy in the book. Do American children books usually refer to the the joys of a sweet melancholy feeling at the lose of something good that will never return? How do children relate to depressed characters? Do they even notice it? Why aren't more children books filled with melancholy, and not in a humorous way? While the sun beat down on my bench, and the film crew near the fountain apparently were also on lunch from filming what looked like a 'dancing in the streets' movie, and the girl sitting the next bench over was losing in a battle of wits against a pigeon who was not going to be fooled into giving up the cracker she dropped I thought about these things. <br /><br />Then I came home and read this book. <br /><br />This book is to the <i>Moomins</i> as finding yourself laying in bed staring at the ceiling at 3am realizing that half your life is over all the good things are past and only pain and regret are ahead of you and having a slightly sad tinged memory of a something wonderful in the past that you will never be able to return to. There is something devastating about this story, and the whole format of the book, from the typesetting and font used to the color of the pages used in the book and the illustrations all work in a harmony to create this strong affect. <br /><br />I'm not sure how kids would react to this, I think from a child's perspective they would only see a story that kind of follows a kind of familiar pattern they are already familiar with. They might find it kind of sad, but nothing too out of the ordinary. Or maybe it will depress them and turn them at a young age to the happy joys of reading literature filled with despair. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8467113249857899923" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780763643676" href="/book/show/6552772-the-miraculous-journey-of-edward-tulane">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/730754-ken?shelf=intermediate-reader">intermediate-reader</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/730754-ken?shelf=finished-in-2020">finished-in-2020</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3266529518" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16847047734670522584">This book got a boost by a write-up of books to read in dark times (read: coronavirus times) in <i>The New York Times</i> (the times, they are a changin'). It's one of those intermediate little kids book that work just fine for adults with a heart, too (or without a heart, if you're feeling like Ebeneezer Scrooge on the verge of the Ghost of Christmas Future).<br/><br/>Literally, it's about a china rabbit, 3 feet tall, with all manner of uppity airs about himelf. The grandmother of his owner, a little girl who l</span>
  <span id="freeText16847047734670522584" style="display:none">This book got a boost by a write-up of books to read in dark times (read: coronavirus times) in <i>The New York Times</i> (the times, they are a changin'). It's one of those intermediate little kids book that work just fine for adults with a heart, too (or without a heart, if you're feeling like Ebeneezer Scrooge on the verge of the Ghost of Christmas Future).<br /><br />Literally, it's about a china rabbit, 3 feet tall, with all manner of uppity airs about himelf. The grandmother of his owner, a little girl who lavishes attention on him, tells a grim, Grimm-like fairy tale of a witch who turns a beautiful (but heartless) princess into a warthog. Then Granny eyes the rabbit and says, quite simply, "You disappoint me." What "A" (fairy tale) has to do with "B" (Granny's disappointment with Edward) is DiCamillo's to know and yours to find out.<br /><br />The rest of the book traces unlikely links between figurative warthogs and literal rabbits. Edward's "journey" provides narrative momentum as he goes from good owner to bad, ocean depths to landfill mountain, female attire (rabbits are a tough call) to sporty red duds. Yes, all that burning up the 200 pages, but there's a much bigger journey happening in his heart. The reader just doesn't know it yet.<br /><br />An allegory about love written with an economy of words is worthy of not only little-kid praise, but big-kid "I-need-a-break: kicking back" praise. Thanks, Edward, for reminding me it only <i>looks</i> like the end of the world. The journey isn't over.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16847047734670522584" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1416936208" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14616479623986961534">I think this book is stunning. It tells the story of a china rabbit named Edward, who is nothing more than a toy in the beginning. A much-loved toy, but he doesn't seem capable of any feeling himself, other than annoyance when he is not treated as he thinks he deserves to be treated. I really did not like Edward at first, he was just so cold-hearted!<br/><br/>But this book is about growth. The journey is both actual and metaphysical. During his wanderings Edward has many experiences which teach him about </span>
  <span id="freeText14616479623986961534" style="display:none">I think this book is stunning. It tells the story of a china rabbit named Edward, who is nothing more than a toy in the beginning. A much-loved toy, but he doesn't seem capable of any feeling himself, other than annoyance when he is not treated as he thinks he deserves to be treated. I really did not like Edward at first, he was just so cold-hearted!<br /><br />But this book is about growth. The journey is both actual and metaphysical. During his wanderings Edward has many experiences which teach him about friendship, love, hope and happiness: he spends time with an adorable old woman and her husband, then with a hobo, then with a little girl who is ill but who cuddles him like a baby and melts his heart.<br /><br />The transitions between each stage of the journey are difficult for Edward, but he does finally arrive exactly where he belongs, in a perfect misty-eyed ending that I should have expected but didn't. <br /><br />I loved the color illustrations, showing Edward in all his glory during each phase of his journey. By the end of the story I loved Edward as if he were my very own. And I can't help it, I will just have to read it again before I put it back in the bookcase.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14616479623986961534" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2685305982" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2685305982" />
    <a title="Linda Hart" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2455252-linda-hart"><img alt="Linda Hart" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1549256566p2/2455252.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2685305982?book_show_action=true">Apr 03, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Linda Hart" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Linda Hart" href="/user/show/2455252-linda-hart">Linda Hart</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2685305982" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5932739366548085440"><br/><br/>I LOVED this sweet, meaningful story so much that as soon as I finished the library's audio version (which is VERY well done) I purchased the hardbound copy for my own library! Edward Tulane is quite the dapper china rabbit, and he knows it. He's loved dearly by Abilene, the little girl who owns and cares for him so carefully, but he has a heart as cold as the china he's made from and loves only himself. Then he abruptly loses his privileged life and finds himself adrift in the real world where </span>
  <span id="freeText5932739366548085440" style="display:none"><br /><br />I LOVED this sweet, meaningful story so much that as soon as I finished the library's audio version (which is VERY well done) I purchased the hardbound copy for my own library! Edward Tulane is quite the dapper china rabbit, and he knows it. He's loved dearly by Abilene, the little girl who owns and cares for him so carefully, but he has a heart as cold as the china he's made from and loves only himself. Then he abruptly loses his privileged life and finds himself adrift in the real world where he must adapt and survive with help from the diverse and memorable people who cross his path. Along the way, Edward is brought low by circumstances, but he learns humility, how to survive, and eventually how to find his way home. <br /><br />Although this may have been written for younger readers I believe it will be enjoyed by people of all ages. If you don't cry at some point you're as heartless as Edward is in the beginning. However, despite the tears, you will be left with a happy ending. I have to admit that I bawled like a baby when I finished this book, a modern classic. <br /><br />This is a WONDERFUL, worthy read, the story will touch your heart, and the illustrations are lovely.<br />Kate DiCamillo is also the author of <u>The Tale of Despereaux</u>, <u>Because of Winn Dixie</u> and over a dozen other books.<br />quotes:<br /><br />“I have been loved, Edward told the stars. So? said the stars. What difference does that make when you are all alone now?” <br />. . . <br /><br />"I'm done with being loved," Edward told her. "I'm done with loving. It's too painful." . . . <br /><br />"Open your heart," she said gently. "Someone will come. Someone will come for you. But first you must open your heart."<br /><br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="5932739366548085440" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3972045-la-coccinelle?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1382286449" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4232190200869916673">I'm not a crier when it comes to books... but this one nearly had me in tears by the end.<br/><br/>My mom read this one herself years ago, long before I'd discovered Kate DiCamillo's books. She enjoyed it, and I sort of filed that information away, along with a vague notion that I should one day give this book a try. Now that I've read it, I really wish I hadn't waited so long. It's probably one of the best reads I've had all year.<br/><br/>DiCamillo has this way of writing for kids that challenges them and respect</span>
  <span id="freeText4232190200869916673" style="display:none">I'm not a crier when it comes to books... but this one nearly had me in tears by the end.<br><br>My mom read this one herself years ago, long before I'd discovered Kate DiCamillo's books. She enjoyed it, and I sort of filed that information away, along with a vague notion that I should one day give this book a try. Now that I've read it, I really wish I hadn't waited so long. It's probably one of the best reads I've had all year.<br><br>DiCamillo has this way of writing for kids that challenges them and respects them as intelligent readers. She's not afraid to use big words or deep concepts. That's the case here, as well, with the story of a proud, somewhat-vain china rabbit who finds himself lost. Throughout his journey, he meets friends and enemies, and all the while he grows as a... well, not a person, but as an intelligent being. He learns to love, only to lose hope when he's separated from the ones he loves over and over again. But, eventually, the love returns, completing the miraculous journey that's just as internal as it is about travelling in the world.<br><br>In some ways, this seems like a fairy tale. In other ways, I was reminded of stories like <i>The Velveteen Rabbit</i>, with the theme of a toy being loved so much that it eventually becomes real. Edward was always "real", though; his journey had more to do with being his best rabbit self.<br><br>The illustrations are the perfect complement to the gentle story. Some are monochromatic, and others are in full colour, but each one helps Edward's world come alive in the mind of the reader.<br><br>I don't know if I can recommend this one enough. It's a great middle-grade read, as it's not too long, but it's perfectly suitable for older readers (including adults) as well. Kate DiCamillo has yet to disappoint me.<br><br><b>Quotable moment:</b><br><br><blockquote>Edward hung by his velvet ears and looked up at the night sky. He saw the stars. But for the first time in his life, he looked at them and felt no comfort. Instead, he felt mocked. You are down there alone, the stars seemed to say to him. And we are up here, in our constellations, together.<br><br>I have been loved, Edward told the stars.<br><br>So? said the stars. What difference does that make when you are all alone now?<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1542061353i/26585071.jpg" width="265" height="320" alt="The Miraculous Journey of Edward Tulane" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</blockquote></span>

<div id="review_14126057" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/14126057?book_show_action=true">Jan 30, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amy" href="/user/show/804350-amy">Amy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780763639877" href="/book/show/2642151-the-miraculous-journey-of-edward-tulane">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/804350-amy?shelf=my-favorites">my-favorites</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer14126057" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2180201927528882384">I have to say that I am quite fond of Kate DiCamillo's writing, and Maggie and I very much enjoyed reading this book together. Oh, but it was exquisitely and simultaneously joyful and sad. Maggie even asked me later last night, after we had finished the book and she was getting ready to brush her teeth, "Why do people write about sad things, Mom?" Ah - innocence slips away in small parcels. Then again, when I read her The Velveteen Rabbit, B actually came running into the room to find out what w</span>
  <span id="freeText2180201927528882384" style="display:none">I have to say that I am quite fond of Kate DiCamillo's writing, and Maggie and I very much enjoyed reading this book together. Oh, but it was exquisitely and simultaneously joyful and sad. Maggie even asked me later last night, after we had finished the book and she was getting ready to brush her teeth, "Why do people write about sad things, Mom?" Ah - innocence slips away in small parcels. Then again, when I read her The Velveteen Rabbit, B actually came running into the room to find out what was the matter because she was so distraught. This equally touching story of a china rabbit is affecting. There is a wonderfully taught fairy tale in the midst of the story, and DiCamillo does not hesitate to leave sadness hanging in the air, strings untied, mean circumstances uncured, and yet somehow comes around to a happy ending. <br /><br />We all hope for the happy ending, but I am more encouraged by one that is imperfect because it is ultimately more believable. It is very readable, and the illustrations are quite nice. It moves quickly, and at Maggie's age it is exciting when you pass page 100 or 150 because it feels like a great novelty. However, if you do not have access to an interested and astute little one to read this to, there is charm to be found in it for everyone. I had to hold back tears when I was reading the last few chapters...and that almost never happens to me. So, either this will tug at your heart strings, or I am just turning into an old fuddy duddy. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2180201927528882384" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/796782565" />
    <a title="Robin Hobb" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17730703-robin-hobb"><img alt="Robin Hobb" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442450400p2/17730703.jpg" /></a>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/796782565?book_show_action=true">Dec 21, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robin Hobb" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robin Hobb" href="/user/show/17730703-robin-hobb">Robin Hobb</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer796782565" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17795665941004317372">This is the harrowing tale of a china rabbit doll who is separated from his young owner and undergoes a series of harrowing adventures over a number of years before finding a happy resolution.<br/><br/>Left to myself, I would put it alongside Black Beauty or Lassie Come Home, tales of animals who endure extended abuse, hardship and/or neglect before being reunited with owners who love them. <br/><br/>When I read those stories in my distant youth, they spoke to me. Now, I fear, if I re-read them, I might suspect tha</span>
  <span id="freeText17795665941004317372" style="display:none">This is the harrowing tale of a china rabbit doll who is separated from his young owner and undergoes a series of harrowing adventures over a number of years before finding a happy resolution.<br /><br />Left to myself, I would put it alongside Black Beauty or Lassie Come Home, tales of animals who endure extended abuse, hardship and/or neglect before being reunited with owners who love them. <br /><br />When I read those stories in my distant youth, they spoke to me. Now, I fear, if I re-read them, I might suspect that the author was deliberately playing with my emotions. Even though I know that authors, do exactly that to make a point.<br /><br />What charmed me about this book was having it read to me in 20 minute segments at night by one of my grand children. And seeing how the book spoke to her much-less-jaded heart, and how she rejoiced in the ending. And hearing the beauty of the 'story telling voice' in this book that does not shrink from a sophisticated vocabulary in choosing the right word over the simpler word.<br /><br />So, this book was a 3 star for me, and 5 star for the child who read it to me. I guess that averages out to 4 stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17795665941004317372" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer7424367078271719696">Edward is sort of like a narcissistic Velveteen Rabbit, unlikeable at the best of times, but when his life turns upside-down he finds himself in a world of sadness and confusion... the only world with true friends, the only world where he can learn the meaning and power of love. This is a book that every child (and adult for that matter!) should get the chance to read, and with its vivid full-color illustrations it really stands out among other children's stories.</span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4146551-kelli?shelf=childrens">childrens</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1189401964" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4647178935559890219">I never review children's books, though I read aloud no less than three a day. This whopper of a book was 200 pages of masterful writing with gorgeous illustrations. I loved everything about this perfect story of a lost toy but what I loved the most was the intricate way that the language of this story was handled. The rabbit thought quite a lot of himself and that was evident in his choice of words...you could read how he felt about himself in the things he said and the way he said them. The la</span>
  <span id="freeText4647178935559890219" style="display:none">I never review children's books, though I read aloud no less than three a day. This whopper of a book was 200 pages of masterful writing with gorgeous illustrations. I loved everything about this perfect story of a lost toy but what I loved the most was the intricate way that the language of this story was handled. The rabbit thought quite a lot of himself and that was evident in his choice of words...you could read how he felt about himself in the things he said and the way he said them. The language was a little high for children but made the story so enjoyable for the reader.<br /><br />Aside from this beautiful love affair with the English language was this lovely, lovely story about the redeeming quality of love...accepting love, suffering love, and healing through love. I was literally incapable of reading the final page aloud and that was the second time in the book that I was choking back tears. Powerful, beautiful, masterful...a book to be treasured.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4647178935559890219" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer3625673617" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer3685883911083199398">I've actually read this book before, it was one of my favorites, and i just wanted to see if I would still like the book as much as I used to. Its an easy and fast read, but hinestly I think its very enjoyable no matter the age. The book is like a life lesson, don't give up on hope or on yourself. I think quite a few people have mentioned that the book is kind of like Toy story, and I agree, but I feel like even though the topic is very similar, this book still towers over toy story. When Edward</span>
  <span id="freeText3685883911083199398" style="display:none">I've actually read this book before, it was one of my favorites, and i just wanted to see if I would still like the book as much as I used to. Its an easy and fast read, but hinestly I think its very enjoyable no matter the age. The book is like a life lesson, don't give up on hope or on yourself. I think quite a few people have mentioned that the book is kind of like Toy story, and I agree, but I feel like even though the topic is very similar, this book still towers over toy story. When Edward is moved around he is by himself, in Toy Story friendship and teamwork is very incorporated. This book teaches that with love that comes, so does separation. Because good things end. Edward's character in general is really well fleshed out and we can feel his emotions. Now for one of the main reasons I love this book, the ending. Maybe it's just me, but I really didn't expect it. I got so attached to Edward that when he was under the weather I just wanted to do anything in my power to help(ahem...obviously I couldn't). I feel like I could've read 200 pages more on how he becomes happy once again, and he accepts the fact that not everything lasts, but we should make the most of it. Nothing has changed through all these years. This book is still great. Its really nostalgic. &gt;^&lt;</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer17851871933476945636">A miraculous journey, indeed.<br/>I just finished the audio version and my second time through this modern parable about learning to love.<br/>Below is my original review and it still stands.<br/><br/>I can be quite a senimental sap at times and I have not cried this hard for joys mingled with regrets since The Last Battle by C.S. Lewis<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11127.The_Chronicles_of_Narnia___1_7_' title='The Chronicles of Narnia (#1-7) by C.S. Lewis' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Chronicles of Narnia</a>.<br/>I read this book aloud with my 7 yr old son. I think Kate DiCamillo is a special writer because she can write about china rabbits being loved by little girls</span>
  <span id="freeText17851871933476945636" style="display:none">A miraculous journey, indeed.<br />I just finished the audio version and my second time through this modern parable about learning to love.<br />Below is my original review and it still stands.<br /><br />I can be quite a senimental sap at times and I have not cried this hard for joys mingled with regrets since The Last Battle by C.S. Lewis<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11127.The_Chronicles_of_Narnia___1_7_" title="The Chronicles of Narnia (#1-7) by C.S. Lewis" rel="nofollow noopener">The Chronicles of Narnia</a>.<br />I read this book aloud with my 7 yr old son. I think Kate DiCamillo is a special writer because she can write about china rabbits being loved by little girls and my son shifts quickly from soft pillow to sitting up rapt waiting for what comes next.<br />I cannot wait to read another of her books. I was in rapt attention myself.<br /><br />This is the story of a china rabbit who is loved by a little girl named Abilene. Edward is well-dressed, has his own pocket watch, gets to sit at the table with the family and thinks quite a lot of himself. He was purchased for Abilene by her grandmother, Pellegrina.<br /><br />One night Pellegrina tells Abilene a story about a princess that is quite full of herself and who is not interested in loving others. The message is not lost on Edward, especially when the woman leans over his bed and says to him "You disappoint me."<br /><br />Fate takes Edward in and out of the lives of many who love him and slowly but surely he begins to understand what Pellegrina was alluding to with her story so long ago.<br /><br />I cried again, when I told my husband all about it. <br /><br />This was a really great book. The illustrations were lovely as well and really captured the quiet, endearing, enduring feel of this book.<br /><br />The story is appropriate for someone as young as 5 or 6, but some of the vocabulary is a bit large. It gave me the opportunity to explain some new concepts as we went.</span>

<div id="review_605972792" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/605972792" />
    <a title="Shaun" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9779623-shaun"><img alt="Shaun" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565572877p2/9779623.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/605972792?book_show_action=true">May 03, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shaun" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shaun" href="/user/show/9779623-shaun">Shaun</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer605972792" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14176328442176104863">I first read a library copy of this book several years ago to my oldest son, after it was recommended to me by my sister-in-law and having previously read Kate DiCamillo's <i>Mercy Watson</i> series for beginning readers as well as <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/503344057' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Tale of Despereaux</a>. <br/><br/>Recently, I picked up a copy of this book along with <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/500736817' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Magician's Elephant</a>, yet another gem by the same author, to have at home for the kiddos.<br/><br/>In between books, I decided to re-read <i>The Miraculous Journey of Edward Tulane</i> while doing my morning walk</span>
  <span id="freeText14176328442176104863" style="display:none">I first read a library copy of this book several years ago to my oldest son, after it was recommended to me by my sister-in-law and having previously read Kate DiCamillo's <i>Mercy Watson</i> series for beginning readers as well as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/503344057" rel="nofollow noopener">The Tale of Despereaux</a>. <br /><br />Recently, I picked up a copy of this book along with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/500736817" rel="nofollow noopener">The Magician's Elephant</a>, yet another gem by the same author, to have at home for the kiddos.<br /><br />In between books, I decided to re-read <i>The Miraculous Journey of Edward Tulane</i> while doing my morning walk on the treadmill. And once again I am in awe of Kate DiCamillo. Her stories are timeless, beautifully written, magical and filled with memorable moments and memorable characters. <br /><br />I often think people underestimate how hard it is to write for children, because let's face it, a really good piece of children's literature not only has to win over its target audience but also has to appeal to adults. This is one I can see myself reading to my grandchildren some day along with many of her other works. Can't say that about many contemporary stories.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14176328442176104863" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('HBer1ubRNa8rEsaKJMCsXSzQvCPECZLQq8XvqnPPeyE16UeBOaOWyszZQRhlgYT3/1Hbpz5432qn+JlzNnOWhA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('UXqQOTZHHH8WFGfFir58qc4pzxG8sKHZ4LOZES78IK54hHxu6TW/GvHf4FfL/1QDHaiolUbB7GPsju/Ia0DNCw==')})
  }
//]]>

Living at the Exit 8 Big Top Mall and Video Arcade, he has grown accustomed to humans watching him through the glass walls of his domain. He rarely misses his life in the jungle. In fact, he hardly ever thinks about it a<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText345485042387367727\" style=\"display:none\">Ivan is an easygoing gorilla. Living at the Exit 8 Big Top Mall and Video Arcade, he has grown accustomed to humans watching him through the glass walls of his domain. He rarely misses his life in the jungle. In fact, he hardly ever thinks about it at all.\n\nInstead, Ivan thinks about TV shows he’s seen and about his friends Stella, an elderly elephant, and Bob, a stray dog. But mostly Ivan thinks about art and how to capture the taste of a mango or the sound of leaves with color and a well-placed line.\n\nThen he meets Ruby, a baby elephant taken from her family, and she makes Ivan see their home—and his own art—through new eyes. When Ruby arrives, change comes with her, and it’s up to Ivan to make it a change for the better.\n\nKatherine Applegate blends humor and poignancy to create Ivan’s unforgettable first-person narration in a story of friendship, art, and hope.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"345485042387367727\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer9101657534427382137">Kate DiCamillo, the newly named National Ambassador for Young People’s Literature for 2014–2015, says about stories, “When we read together, we connect. Together, we see the world. Together, we see one another.” Born in Philadelphia, the author lives in Minneapolis, where she faithfully writes two pages a day, five days a week.<br/><br/> Kate DiCamillo's own journey is something of a dream come true. After </span>
  <span id="freeText9101657534427382137" style="display:none">Kate DiCamillo, the newly named National Ambassador for Young People’s Literature for 2014–2015, says about stories, “When we read together, we connect. Together, we see the world. Together, we see one another.” Born in Philadelphia, the author lives in Minneapolis, where she faithfully writes two pages a day, five days a week.<br /><br /> Kate DiCamillo's own journey is something of a dream come true. After moving to Minnesota from Florida in her twenties, homesickness and a bitter winter helped inspire Because of Winn-Dixie - her first published novel, which, remarkably, became a runaway bestseller and snapped up a Newbery Honor. "After the Newbery committee called me, I spent the whole day walking into walls," she says. "I was stunned. And very, very happy."<br /><br />Her second novel, The Tiger Rising, went on to become a National Book Award Finalist. Since then, the master storyteller has written for a wide range of ages, including two comical early-chapter-book series - Mercy Watson, which stars a "porcine wonder" with an obsession for buttered toast, and Bink &amp; Gollie, which celebrates the tall and short of a marvelous friendship - as well as a luminous holiday picture book, Great Joy.<br /><br />Her latest novel, Flora &amp; Ulysses: The Illuminated Adventures, won the 2014 Newbery Medal. It was released in fall 2013 to great acclaim, including five starred reviews, and was an instant New York Times bestseller. Flora &amp; Ulysses is a laugh-out-loud story filled with eccentric, endearing characters and featuring an exciting new format - a novel interspersed with comic-style graphic sequences and full-page illustrations, all rendered in black and white by up-and-coming artist K. G. Campbell. It was a 2013 Parents' Choice Gold Award Winner and was chosen by Amazon, Publishers Weekly, Kirkus Reviews, and Common Sense Media as a Best Book of the Year.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9101657534427382137" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
